<div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JamesKPolkHome.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/JamesKPolkHome.jpg/310px-JamesKPolkHome.jpg" width="310" height="274" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/JamesKPolkHome.jpg/465px-JamesKPolkHome.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/JamesKPolkHome.jpg/620px-JamesKPolkHome.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2137" data-file-height="1888" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JamesKPolkHome.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The house where Polk spent his adult life before his presidency, in <a href="/wiki/Columbia,_Tennessee" title="Columbia, Tennessee">Columbia, Tennessee</a>, is his only private residence still standing. It is now known as the <a href="/wiki/James_K._Polk_Ancestral_Home" title="James K. Polk Ancestral Home">James K. Polk Ancestral Home</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>James Knox Polk</b> (November 2, 1795 – June 15, 1849) was the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">11th President of the United States</a> (1845–49). He previously served as the 13th <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the United States House of Representatives">Speaker of the House of Representatives</a> and as <a href="/wiki/List_of_Governors_of_Tennessee" title="List of Governors of Tennessee">Governor of Tennessee</a>. A protege of <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>, Polk was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> and an adherent of <a href="/wiki/Jacksonian_democracy" title="Jacksonian democracy">Jacksonian democracy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manifest_Destiny" class="mw-redirect" title="Manifest Destiny">Manifest Destiny</a>. During his presidency, the United States expanded significantly with the <a href="/wiki/Annexation_of_Texas" class="mw-redirect" title="Annexation of Texas">annexation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Texas" title="Republic of Texas">Republic of Texas</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Treaty" title="Oregon Treaty">Oregon Treaty</a>, and the conclusion of the <a href="/wiki/Mexican-American_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Mexican-American War">Mexican-American War</a>.</p>
<p>Polk was born in <a href="/wiki/Pineville,_North_Carolina" title="Pineville, North Carolina">Pineville, North Carolina</a>, and moved to <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a> to study law. After building a successful law practice, he was elected to the Tennessee legislature and then to the United States House of Representatives in 1825. A close ally of President Andrew Jackson, Polk served as the Speaker of the House of Representatives from 1835 to 1839, making him the only president to have also served as Speaker. He left Congress to serve as Governor of Tennessee from 1839 to 1841. After losing re-election as governor in 1840, and losing in another gubernatorial election in 1842, Polk was a <a href="/wiki/Dark_horse" title="Dark horse">dark horse</a> candidate for president in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1844" title="United States presidential election, 1844">1844</a>. Though he entered the convention hoping to be nominated for vice president, he won the presidential nomination as a compromise candidate among the various party factions. In the general election, he defeated <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a> of the rival <a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">Whig Party</a> in large part due to his promise to annex the Republic of Texas.</p>
<p>Polk is often considered the last strong pre–<a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a> president, having met during his four years in office every major domestic and foreign policy goal set during his campaign and the transition to his administration. He threatened war with the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom</a> over the disputed <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Country" title="Oregon Country">Oregon Country</a>, eventually reaching a settlement in which the United States and Britain divided the territory along the 49th parallel. After <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> rejected the annexation of Texas by the United States, Polk achieved a sweeping victory in the Mexican–American War, which resulted in the cession by Mexico of nearly the whole of what is now the <a href="/wiki/Southwestern_United_States" title="Southwestern United States">American Southwest</a>. He ensured a substantial reduction of <a href="/wiki/Tariffs_in_United_States_history" title="Tariffs in United States history">tariff</a> rates by replacing the "<a href="/wiki/Black_Tariff" class="mw-redirect" title="Black Tariff">Black Tariff</a>" with the <a href="/wiki/Walker_tariff" title="Walker tariff">Walker tariff</a> of 1846, which pleased the less-industrialized states of his native South by rendering less expensive both imported and, through competition, domestic goods. In addition, he re-established the <a href="/wiki/Independent_Treasury" title="Independent Treasury">Independent Treasury</a> System, oversaw the opening of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Academy" title="United States Naval Academy">United States Naval Academy</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a>, the groundbreaking for the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Monument" title="Washington Monument">Washington Monument</a>, and the issuance of the first United States <a href="/wiki/Postage_stamp" title="Postage stamp">postage stamp</a>. True to his campaign pledge to serve only one term as president, Polk left office in March 1849 and returned to Tennessee. He died of <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> three months later.</p>
<p>Scholars have ranked him favorably on <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_United_States_Presidents" class="mw-redirect" title="Historical rankings of United States Presidents">lists of greatest presidents</a> for his ability to promote, obtain support for, and achieve all of the major items on his presidential agenda. However, he has also been criticized for leading the country into war against Mexico and for exacerbating sectional divides. Polk has been called the "least known consequential president".<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

</div>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_K._Polk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>James Knox Polk, the first of ten children, was born on November 2, 1795 in a <a href="/wiki/Log_cabin" title="Log cabin">log house</a><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> in what is now <a href="/wiki/Pineville,_North_Carolina" title="Pineville, North Carolina">Pineville, North Carolina</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mecklenburg_County,_North_Carolina" title="Mecklenburg County, North Carolina">Mecklenburg County</a>, just outside <a href="/wiki/Charlotte,_North_Carolina" title="Charlotte, North Carolina">Charlotte</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman6_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman6-3">[3]</a></sup> (<i>Further information: <a href="/wiki/President_James_K._Polk_Historic_Site" title="President James K. Polk Historic Site">President James K. Polk Historic Site</a></i>) His father, Samuel Polk, was a slaveholder, successful farmer and <a href="/wiki/Surveyor_(surveying)" class="mw-redirect" title="Surveyor (surveying)">surveyor</a> of <a href="/wiki/Scotch-Irish_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Scotch-Irish American">Scots-Irish</a> descent. His mother, Jane Polk (née Knox), was a descendant of a brother of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scottish</a> religious reformer <a href="/wiki/John_Knox" title="John Knox">John Knox</a>. She named her firstborn after her father James Knox.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman6_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman6-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Like many early Scots-Irish settlers in the North Carolina mountains, the Knox and Polk families were <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterian">Presbyterian</a>. While Polk's mother Jane remained a devout Presbyterian her entire life, his father Samuel (whose own father, <a href="/wiki/Ezekiel_Polk" title="Ezekiel Polk">Ezekiel Polk</a>, was a <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deist</a>) rejected dogmatic Presbyterianism. When the parents took the young James to a Presbyterian church to be baptized, the father Samuel refused to declare his belief in Christianity, and the minister refused to baptize the child.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman6_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman6-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-haynes2_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haynes2-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1803, most of Polk's relatives moved to the <a href="/wiki/Duck_River_(Tennessee)" title="Duck River (Tennessee)">Duck River</a> area in what is now <a href="/wiki/Maury_County,_Tennessee" title="Maury County, Tennessee">Maury County</a>, Middle Tennessee; Polk's family waited until 1806 to follow.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman7_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman7-5">[5]</a></sup> The family grew prosperous, with Samuel Polk turning to land speculation and becoming a county judge.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman7_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman7-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Polk was home schooled.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman7_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman7-5">[5]</a></sup> His health was problematic and in 1812 his pain became so unbearable that he was taken to Dr. <a href="/wiki/Ephraim_McDowell" title="Ephraim McDowell">Ephraim McDowell</a> of <a href="/wiki/Danville,_Kentucky" title="Danville, Kentucky">Danville, Kentucky</a>, who operated to remove <a href="/wiki/Bladder_stone" title="Bladder stone">urinary stones</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman8_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman8-6">[6]</a></sup> Polk was awake during the operation with nothing but <a href="/wiki/Brandy" title="Brandy">brandy</a> available for <a href="/wiki/Anesthetic" title="Anesthetic">anesthetic</a>, but it was successful. The surgery may have left Polk <a href="/wiki/Infertility" title="Infertility">sterile</a>, as he did not sire any children.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>When Polk recovered, his father offered to bring him into the mercantile business, but Polk refused.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman8_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman8-6">[6]</a></sup> In July 1813, Polk enrolled at the <a href="/wiki/Zion_Presbyterian_Church_(Tennessee)" class="mw-redirect" title="Zion Presbyterian Church (Tennessee)">Zion Church</a> near his home. A year later he attended an academy in <a href="/wiki/Murfreesboro,_Tennessee" title="Murfreesboro, Tennessee">Murfreesboro</a>, where he may have met his future wife, <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Childress_Polk" title="Sarah Childress Polk">Sarah Childress</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman13_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman13-8">[8]</a></sup> At Murfreesboro, Polk proved a promising student.</p>
<p>In January 1816, Polk was admitted into the <a href="/wiki/University_of_North_Carolina_at_Chapel_Hill" title="University of North Carolina at Chapel Hill">University of North Carolina</a> as a second-semester sophomore. The Polk family had connections with the university, then a small school of about 80 students: Sam Polk was their land agent for Tennessee, and his cousin, <a href="/wiki/William_Polk_(colonel)" title="William Polk (colonel)">William Polk</a>, was a trustee.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> While there, Polk joined the <a href="/wiki/Dialectic_and_Philanthropic_Societies" title="Dialectic and Philanthropic Societies">Dialectic Society</a> where he regularly took part in debates and learned the art of <a href="/wiki/Eloquence" title="Eloquence">oratory</a>. His roommate <a href="/wiki/William_Dunn_Moseley" title="William Dunn Moseley">William Dunn Moseley</a> later became the first governor of Florida. Polk graduated with honors in May 1818.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman9_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman9-10">[10]</a></sup> The University later named the lower quad on its main campus, Polk Place.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>After his graduation from the University of North Carolina, Polk traveled to <a href="/wiki/Nashville,_Tennessee" title="Nashville, Tennessee">Nashville</a> to <a href="/wiki/Reading_law" title="Reading law">study law</a> under renowned Nashville trial attorney <a href="/wiki/Felix_Grundy" title="Felix Grundy">Felix Grundy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman10_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman10-12">[12]</a></sup> Grundy became Polk's first mentor. On September 20, 1819, Polk, with Grundy's endorsement, was elected clerk for the <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_State_Senate" class="mw-redirect" title="Tennessee State Senate">Tennessee State Senate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman11_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman11-13">[13]</a></sup> Polk was re-elected as clerk in 1821 without opposition, and would continue to serve until 1822. Polk was <a href="/wiki/Admission_to_the_bar_in_the_United_States" title="Admission to the bar in the United States">licensed</a> to practice law in June 1820. His first case was to defend his father against a public fighting charge. He secured his client's release for a one-dollar fine.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman11_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman11-13">[13]</a></sup> Polk's practice was successful, in large part due to the many cases arising from debts after the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1819" title="Panic of 1819">Panic of 1819</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_political_career">Early political career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_K._Polk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early political career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 1822 Polk joined the Tennessee militia as a captain in the cavalry regiment of the 5th Brigade. He was later appointed a colonel on the staff of Governor <a href="/wiki/William_Carroll_(Tennessee_politician)" title="William Carroll (Tennessee politician)">William Carroll</a>, and was afterwards often referred to or addressed by his military title.<sup id="cite_ref-s25_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-s25-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Polk's oratory became popular, earning him the nickname "Napoleon of the Stump." In 1822 Polk resigned his position as clerk to run his successful campaign for the Tennessee state legislature in 1823, in which he defeated incumbent William Yancey, becoming the new representative of Maury County.<sup id="cite_ref-s25_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-s25-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Borneman14_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman14-17">[17]</a></sup> In October 1823 Polk voted for <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>, a family friend and fellow Tennessean,<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> to become the next United States Senator from Tennessee.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman17_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman17-19">[19]</a></sup> Jackson won and Polk became a strong supporter of Jackson's political career and an advocate for <a href="/wiki/Jacksonian_democracy" title="Jacksonian democracy">Jackson's ideas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman18_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman18-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_K_Polk_and_Sarah_C_Polk.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/James_K_Polk_and_Sarah_C_Polk.jpg/250px-James_K_Polk_and_Sarah_C_Polk.jpg" width="250" height="309" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/James_K_Polk_and_Sarah_C_Polk.jpg/375px-James_K_Polk_and_Sarah_C_Polk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6a/James_K_Polk_and_Sarah_C_Polk.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="495" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_K_Polk_and_Sarah_C_Polk.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
James K. Polk and Sarah Childress Polk</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Polk courted <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Childress" class="mw-redirect" title="Sarah Childress">Sarah Childress</a>, and they married on January 1, 1824 in Murfreesboro.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman14_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman14-17">[17]</a></sup> Polk was then 28, and Sarah was 20 years old. They had no children. During Polk's political career, Sarah assisted her husband with his speeches, gave him advice on policy matters and played an active role in his campaigns. An old story told that Andrew Jackson had encouraged their romance when they began to court.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1824" title="United States presidential election, 1824">1824</a>, Jackson ran for President but was defeated.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman22_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman22-23">[23]</a></sup> Though Jackson had won the <a href="/wiki/Direct_election" title="Direct election">popular vote</a>, neither he nor any of the other candidates (<a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a>, and <a href="/wiki/William_H._Crawford" title="William H. Crawford">William H. Crawford</a>) had won a majority of the electoral vote. The House of Representatives then had to select the verdict; Clay, who had received the least amount of electoral votes and dropped from the ballot, supported Adams.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman22_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman22-23">[23]</a></sup> Clay's support proved to be the deciding factor in the House and Adams was elected President.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman22_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman22-23">[23]</a></sup> Adams then offered Clay a position in the Cabinet as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a>, which Clay accepted.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman22_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman22-23">[23]</a></sup> Jackson and Polk strongly criticized the sequence of events. The incident became known as the "<a href="/wiki/Corrupt_Bargain" title="Corrupt Bargain">Corrupt Bargain</a>", in which Clay allegedly violated the will of the people by giving Adams the presidency in return for the State Department.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1825, Polk ran for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Tennessee%27s_6th_congressional_district" title="Tennessee's 6th congressional district">Tennessee's 6th congressional district</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman23_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman23-25">[25]</a></sup> Polk campaigned so vigorously that Sarah began to worry about his health.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman23_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman23-25">[25]</a></sup> During the campaign, Polk's opponents said that at the age of 29 Polk was too young for a spot in the House, but he won the election and took his seat in Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman23_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman23-25">[25]</a></sup> When Polk arrived in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a> he roomed in Benjamin Burch's boarding house with some other Tennessee representatives, including <a href="/wiki/Sam_Houston" title="Sam Houston">Sam Houston</a>. Polk made his first major speech on March 13, 1826, in which he said that the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" title="Electoral College (United States)">Electoral College</a> should be abolished and that the President should be elected by the popular vote.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman24_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman24-26">[26]</a></sup> Polk became a vocal critic of the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_John_Quincy_Adams" title="Presidency of John Quincy Adams">Adams administration</a>, frequently voting against its policies.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Polk won re-election in 1827, and continued to oppose the Adams administration.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman26_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman26-28">[28]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1828" title="United States presidential election, 1828">1828</a>, Jackson ran for President again and during the campaign Polk and Jackson corresponded, with Polk giving Jackson advice on his campaign. Following Jackson's victory in the 1828 election, Polk became one of Jackson's most important and loyal supporters in the House.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Polk successfully opposed federally-funded "<a href="/wiki/Internal_improvements" title="Internal improvements">internal improvements</a>" (infrastructure projects) such as a proposed Buffalo-to-New Orleans road, and he was pleased by Jackson's <a href="/wiki/Maysville_Road_veto" title="Maysville Road veto">Maysville Road veto</a> in May 1830.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Nullification_Crisis" title="Nullification Crisis">Nullification Crisis</a>, Polk sympathized with <a href="/wiki/John_C._Calhoun" title="John C. Calhoun">John C. Calhoun</a>'s opposition to tariffs, but Polk condemned secession and supported the <a href="/wiki/Force_Bill" title="Force Bill">Force Bill</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> In 1833, after being elected to a fifth consecutive term, Polk became the chair of the House Ways and Means Committee.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman33_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman33-32">[32]</a></sup> In this role, Polk served as Jackson's most prominent House ally in the "<a href="/wiki/Bank_War" title="Bank War">Bank War</a>" that developed over Jackson's opposition to the re-authorization of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Bank_of_the_United_States" title="Second Bank of the United States">Second Bank of the United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry4243_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry4243-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Speaker_of_the_House">Speaker of the House</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_K._Polk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Speaker of the House">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In June 1834, Speaker of the House <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Stevenson" title="Andrew Stevenson">Andrew Stevenson</a> resigned from Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman34_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman34-34">[34]</a></sup> With Jackson's support, Polk ran for Speaker against fellow Tennessean <a href="/wiki/John_Bell_(Tennessee_politician)" title="John Bell (Tennessee politician)">John Bell</a>, Calhounite <a href="/wiki/Richard_Henry_Wilde" title="Richard Henry Wilde">Richard Henry Wilde</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Joel_Barlow_Sutherland" title="Joel Barlow Sutherland">Joel Barlow Sutherland</a> of Pennsylvania. After ten ballots, Bell defeated Polk, who was opposed by followers of both Calhoun and <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Biddle_(banker)" title="Nicholas Biddle (banker)">Nicholas Biddle</a>, the president of the Bank of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Bell's election marked the beginning of a rising anti-Jacksonian force in Tennessee, led by Bell and Senator <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Lawson_White" title="Hugh Lawson White">Hugh Lawson White</a>, both erstwhile allies of Jackson.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> In 1835, Polk defeated Bell in a re-match for the speaker office.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman35_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman35-37">[37]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1836" title="United States presidential election, 1836">1836 presidential election</a>, Vice President <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Martin Van Buren</a>, Jackson's chosen successor, defeated multiple <a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">Whig</a> candidates, including Hugh Lawson White.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>As speaker, Polk worked for the policies of Jackson and, after 1836, Van Buren. Polk appointed committees with Democratic chairs and majorities, including the New York radical <a href="/wiki/Churchill_C._Cambreleng" title="Churchill C. Cambreleng">C. C. Cambreleng</a> as Chair of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Ways_and_Means" title="United States House Committee on Ways and Means">Ways and Means Committee</a>, although he maintained the facade of traditional bipartisanship. The two major issues during Polk's speakership were slavery and, after the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1837" title="Panic of 1837">Panic of 1837</a>, the economy. Polk took advantage of the "<a href="/wiki/Gag_rule" title="Gag rule">gag rule</a>" to quiet the slavery debate within the House. Van Buren and Polk faced pressure to rescind the <a href="/wiki/Specie_Circular" title="Specie Circular">Specie Circular</a>, an act that had been signed by Jackson to boost the economy. The act required that payment for government lands be in gold and silver. However, with support from Polk and his cabinet, Van Buren chose to stick with the Specie Circular. Polk and Van Buren attempted to establish an Independent Treasury system that would allow the government to oversee its own deposits (rather than using national or "<a href="/wiki/Pet_bank" class="mw-redirect" title="Pet bank">pet banks</a>"), but the bill was defeated in the House.<sup id="cite_ref-Seigenthaler5760_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seigenthaler5760-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>Polk attempted to make a more orderly house. Unlike many of his peers, he never challenged anyone to a duel no matter how much they insulted his honor.<sup id="cite_ref-Seigenthaler62_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seigenthaler62-40">[40]</a></sup> After seven terms in the House and two as speaker, Polk announced that he would not seek re-election, choosing instead to run for <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Tennessee" title="Governor of Tennessee">Governor of Tennessee</a> in 1839.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Polk remains the only president who served as Speaker of the House.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Governor_of_Tennessee">Governor of Tennessee</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_K._Polk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Governor of Tennessee">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1835, Democrats had lost the governorship of Tennessee for the first time in the party's history, and Polk decided to return home to help the state party. In the 1839 Tennessee gubernatorial election, Polk defeated two-term incumbent Whig <a href="/wiki/Newton_Cannon" title="Newton Cannon">Newton Cannon</a> by about 2,500 votes, out of about 105,000 total votes. Polk and Jackson hoped that Polk's election would serve as a springboard to the vice presidential nomination in 1840 and the presidency in 1844, but the <a href="/wiki/1840_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1840 Democratic National Convention">1840 Democratic National Convention</a> split among Polk and incumbent <a href="/wiki/Richard_Mentor_Johnson" title="Richard Mentor Johnson">Richard Mentor Johnson</a> and failed to nominate anyone for the position.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Polk's three major programs during his governorship; regulating state banks, implementing state internal improvements, and improving education all failed to win the approval of the legislature.<sup id="cite_ref-Seigenthaler66_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seigenthaler66-43">[43]</a></sup> Polk's only major success as governor was the replacement of Tennessee's two Whig Senators with two Democrats.<sup id="cite_ref-Seigenthaler66_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seigenthaler66-43">[43]</a></sup> Polk's tenure was hindered by the continuing nationwide economic crisis that had followed the Panic of 1837, and Van Buren lost the <a href="/wiki/U.S._presidential_election,_1840" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. presidential election, 1840">presidential election of 1840</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Polk sought re-election in 1841 against the Whig candidate, <a href="/wiki/James_C._Jones" title="James C. Jones">James C. Jones</a>. Jones, an entertaining story teller who had emerged in the legislature as one of Polk's most persistent opponents, defeated Polk by 3,243 votes.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry4749_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry4749-45">[45]</a></sup> Polk challenged Jones to a re-match in 1843, campaigning across the state and publicly debating against Jones, but was defeated again, this time by a slightly greater margin of 3,833 votes.<sup id="cite_ref-Borneman_p.64_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borneman_p.64-46">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Seigenthaler68_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seigenthaler68-47">[47]</a></sup> Polk was stung by the first two losses of his political career, and faced an uncertain political future.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry4749_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry4749-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1844">Election of 1844</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_K._Polk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Election of 1844">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1844" title="United States presidential election, 1844">United States presidential election, 1844</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Polk_Dallas_campaign_banner.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Polk_Dallas_campaign_banner.jpg/290px-Polk_Dallas_campaign_banner.jpg" width="290" height="406" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Polk_Dallas_campaign_banner.jpg/435px-Polk_Dallas_campaign_banner.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Polk_Dallas_campaign_banner.jpg/580px-Polk_Dallas_campaign_banner.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1373" data-file-height="1923" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Polk_Dallas_campaign_banner.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1844 campaign banner, produced by <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Currier" title="Nathaniel Currier">Nathaniel Currier</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following his 1843 gubernatorial loss, Polk became determined to become the next <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">Vice President of the United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry_p._43-44_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry_p._43-44-48">[48]</a></sup> Van Buren was the frontrunner for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1844" title="United States presidential election, 1844">1844</a> Democratic nomination, and Polk engaged in a delicate and subtle campaign to become Van Buren's running mate.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry5053_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry5053-49">[49]</a></sup> Though Van Buren had the stature of a former president and was widely seen as the frontrunner, he faced opposition from Southerners who feared his views on slavery, while Van Buren's favorable position on <a href="/wiki/Hard_money_(policy)" title="Hard money (policy)">hard money</a> and his handling of the Panic of 1837 also aroused opposition from "entrepreneurial conservatives."<sup id="cite_ref-Merry5053_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry5053-49">[49]</a></sup> Many Southerners supported the candidacy of former Vice President <a href="/wiki/John_C._Calhoun" title="John C. Calhoun">John C. Calhoun</a> of South Carolina, the entrepreneurial conservatives rallied around Senator <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Cass" title="Lewis Cass">Lewis Cass</a> of Michigan, and former Vice President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Mentor_Johnson" title="Richard Mentor Johnson">Richard Mentor Johnson</a> also maintained a strong following among Democrats.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry5053_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry5053-49">[49]</a></sup> A key aspect of Polk's strategy to become vice president was winning control of the Tennessee delegation to the <a href="/wiki/1844_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1844 Democratic National Convention">1844 Democratic National Convention</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry5355_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry5355-50">[50]</a></sup> Polk defeated a challenge for the state party's leadership by former Senator <a href="/wiki/Alfred_O._P._Nicholson" title="Alfred O. P. Nicholson">Alfred O. P. Nicholson</a>, and Polk instructed the Tennessee delegates to support his vice presidential nomination while remaining neutral on the presidency, thus opening up the possibility of a deal between Polk and Van Buren in which Van Buren would choose Polk as his running mate in exchange for the Tennessee delegation's votes.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry5355_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry5355-50">[50]</a></sup> Polk's maneuvering was opposed at the national level by two powerful Senators, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hart_Benton_(senator)" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Hart Benton (senator)">Thomas Hart Benton</a> of Missouri and <a href="/wiki/James_Buchanan" title="James Buchanan">James Buchanan</a> of Pennsylvania, both of whom sought a vice president of a lesser stature so as to clear the way for their own respective presidential candidacies in 1848.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The potential <a href="/wiki/Texas_annexation" title="Texas annexation">annexation</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Texas" title="Republic of Texas">Republic of Texas</a> by President <a href="/wiki/John_Tyler" title="John Tyler">John Tyler</a> upended the presidential race; while Van Buren and the Whig frontrunner, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a>, opposed the annexation and a potential war with <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> over the disputed territory,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Polk and Andrew Jackson strongly supported territorial acquisition.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Disappointed by Van Buren's position, Jackson instead decided to support Polk as the party's presidential candidate in the 1844 election, though Polk was skeptical that he could win that nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> However, as Van Buren continued to receive opposition from the West and South, Polk's key lieutenant at the convention, <a href="/wiki/Gideon_Johnson_Pillow" title="Gideon Johnson Pillow">Gideon Johnson Pillow</a>, noted the possibility of Polk emerging as a compromise presidential nominee.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the convention began on May 27, 1844, the key question was whether the convention would adopt a rule requiring the presidential nominee to receive the vote of 2/3 of the delegates.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> With the strong support of the Southern states, the two thirds rule was passed by the convention, effectively ending the possibility of Van Buren's nomination due to the strong opposition he faced.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry8788_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry8788-57">[57]</a></sup> Van Buren won a majority on the first presidential ballot, but failed to win the necessary supermajority, and support for Van Buren faded on subsequent ballots.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry8788_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry8788-57">[57]</a></sup> Cass, Johnson, Calhoun, and Buchanan also received votes on the first ballot, and Cass took the lead after the fifth ballot.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> As the balloting continued, anti-Cass forces from the Northeast led by <a href="/wiki/George_Bancroft" title="George Bancroft">George Bancroft</a> rallied support for Polk.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> On the eighth presidential ballot, Polk won 44 of the 266 delegates, as support for all candidates other than Polk, Cass, and Van Buren dissipated.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry9294_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry9294-60">[60]</a></sup> Following the eighth ballot, several delegates rose to give supportive speeches of Polk's candidacy, and Polk won the presidential nomination on the ninth ballot.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry9294_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry9294-60">[60]</a></sup> After Senator <a href="/wiki/Silas_Wright" title="Silas Wright">Silas Wright</a> of New York, a close Van Buren ally, declined the vice presidential nomination, the convention nominated former Senator <a href="/wiki/George_M._Dallas" title="George M. Dallas">George M. Dallas</a> of Pennsylvania.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Polk had not attended the convention, and when he learned of his nomination, Polk stated: "It has been well observed that the office of President of the United States should neither be sought nor declined. I have never sought it, nor should I feel at liberty to decline it, if conferred upon me by the voluntary <a href="/wiki/Suffrage" title="Suffrage">suffrages</a> of my fellow citizens." Because the Democratic Party was splintered into bitter factions, Polk promised to serve only one term if elected, hoping that his disappointed rival Democrats would unite behind him with the knowledge that another candidate would be chosen in four years.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> The Whigs publicly scorned Polk with the chant "Who is James K. Polk?" in reference to Polk's relative obscurity.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry9697_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry9697-63">[63]</a></sup> Though Polk had experience as Speaker of the House and Governor of Tennessee, all previous successful presidential nominees had served as Vice President, Secretary of State, or as a high-ranking general. Polk has been described as the first "<a href="/wiki/Dark_horse" title="Dark horse">dark horse</a>" presidential nominee, but his nomination was less of a surprise than that of many future presidential nominees such as <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Pierce" title="Franklin Pierce">Franklin Pierce</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Despite his party's gibes, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a>, the Whig presidential nominee and himself a former Secretary of State, recognized that Polk could unify the Democrats for the 1844 election.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry9697_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry9697-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1844_Electoral_Map.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/1844_Electoral_Map.png/350px-1844_Electoral_Map.png" width="350" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/1844_Electoral_Map.png/525px-1844_Electoral_Map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/1844_Electoral_Map.png/700px-1844_Electoral_Map.png 2x" data-file-width="867" data-file-height="635" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1844_Electoral_Map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Results of the 1844 Presidential election</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Incumbent President <a href="/wiki/John_Tyler" title="John Tyler">John Tyler</a>—a former Democrat and Whig—had been expelled from the Whig Party in September 1841. Despite Polk's support of the annexation of Texas, Tyler considered running an independent re-election campaign, but he agreed to withdraw after Jackson encouraged Democrats to welcome Tyler's followers into the party.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> With the threat of a Tyler candidacy averted, Polk focused his efforts on unifying the Democratic Party. When he officially accepted the nomination, Polk announced that he would serve only one term.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry103104_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry103104-66">[66]</a></sup> Polk hoped that this would win him the backing of Democratic leaders such as Cass, Calhoun, Benton, Buchanan, and Silas Wright, all of whom were interested in seeking the presidency in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1848" title="United States presidential election, 1848">election of 1848</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry103104_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry103104-66">[66]</a></sup> Polk avoided taking a position on the <a href="/wiki/Protectionist" class="mw-redirect" title="Protectionist">protectionist</a> <a href="/wiki/Tariff_of_1842" title="Tariff of 1842">Tariff of 1842</a>, but appealed to the key state of Pennsylvania by using rhetoric favorable towards tariffs.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry99100_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry99100-67">[67]</a></sup> In New York, another key swing state, Polk's campaign was greatly aided by the gubernatorial candidacy of Wright, who managed to unite the factions of the New York Democratic Party.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry99100_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry99100-67">[67]</a></sup> The potential annexation of Texas also threatened to divide the party between North and South, but Polk managed to appease most Southern party leaders without antagonizing Northern party leaders.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry107108_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry107108-68">[68]</a></sup> As the election drew closer, it became clear that most of the country favored the annexation of Texas, and some Southern Whig leaders supported Polk's campaign due to Clay's anti-annexation stance.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry107108_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry107108-68">[68]</a></sup> In June 1844, Clay attempted to hedge his position on annexation, but this attempt alienated anti-annexation Northerners while doing little to appease pro-annexation forces.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry109111_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry109111-69">[69]</a></sup> Polk won the election with 49.5% of the popular vote and 170 of the 275 electoral votes.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry109111_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry109111-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>Polk lost both his birth state, North Carolina, and his state of residence, Tennessee, the only successful presidential candidate to do so, until <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> lost New York in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2016" title="United States presidential election, 2016">2016</a>. However, he won Pennsylvania and New York, where Clay lost votes to the antislavery Liberty Party candidate <a href="/wiki/James_G._Birney" title="James G. Birney">James G. Birney</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry109111_69-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry109111-69">[69]</a></sup> Also contributing to Polk's victory was the support of new immigrant voters, who opposed the Whigs' policies. Polk won the popular vote by a margin of about 39,000 out of 2.6&#160;million, and took the Electoral College with 170 votes to Clay's 105.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Polk won 15 states, while Clay won 11.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>After being informed of his victory on November 15, 1844, Polk turned his attention towards forming a geographically-balanced Cabinet.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry112113_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry112113-72">[72]</a></sup> Polk decided that his Cabinet would not consist of presidential aspirants, though he chose to nominate Buchanan for the crucial and prestigious position of Secretary of State.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry114117_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry114117-73">[73]</a></sup> Polk wrote Van Buren and Wright with the intention of appointing an acceptable New Yorker to the position of Secretary of the Treasury, and the two recommended <a href="/wiki/Azariah_C._Flagg" title="Azariah C. Flagg">Azariah C. Flagg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry117119_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry117119-74">[74]</a></sup> Polk decided to nominate Senator <a href="/wiki/Robert_J._Walker" title="Robert J. Walker">Robert J. Walker</a> of Mississippi to the position of Attorney General.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry117119_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry117119-74">[74]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cave_Johnson" title="Cave Johnson">Cave Johnson</a>, a close friend and ally of Polk, would be nominated for the position of Postmaster General.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry117119_74-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry117119-74">[74]</a></sup> Though Polk was personally close with the incumbent Navy Secretary, <a href="/wiki/John_Y._Mason" title="John Y. Mason">John Y. Mason</a>, Polk sought to replace him due to Jackson's insistence that none of Tyler's Cabinet be retained.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry117119_74-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry117119-74">[74]</a></sup> In his place, Polk would nominate George Bancroft, the historian who had placed a crucial role in Polk's nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry117119_74-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry117119-74">[74]</a></sup> For the position of Secretary of War, Polk sought to appoint <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Stevenson" title="Andrew Stevenson">Andrew Stevenson</a> of Virginia, giving Polk's potential Cabinet three slave state leaders and three free state leaders.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry117119_74-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry117119-74">[74]</a></sup> Polk's Cabinet choices met with the approval of Andrew Jackson, who Polk met with in January 1845 for the last time, as Jackson would die in June 1845.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry117119_74-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry117119-74">[74]</a></sup> However, after news of Buchanan's selection for State was leaked, Vice President Dallas (an in-state rival of Buchanan) and a slew of Southerners insisted that Walker receive the higher position at Treasury.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry124126_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry124126-75">[75]</a></sup> Polk instead chose to nominate Bancroft as a compromise at Treasury while nominating Mason as Attorney General and a New Yorker, <a href="/wiki/William_L._Marcy" title="William L. Marcy">William L. Marcy</a>, as Secretary of War.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry124126_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry124126-75">[75]</a></sup> Polk had intended the Marcy appointment to mollify Van Buren, but Van Buren was outraged at the move, in part due to Marcy's affiliation with the rival "<a href="/wiki/Barnburners_and_Hunkers" title="Barnburners and Hunkers">Hunker</a>" faction.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry124126_75-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry124126-75">[75]</a></sup> Polk then further enraged Van Buren by finally choosing to elevate Walker to Treasury, and Van Buren and Silas Wright became alienated from the incoming Polk administration.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry127_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry127-76">[76]</a></sup> Thus, Polk's initial Cabinet selections would consist of Buchanan as Secretary of State, Walker as Secretary of the Treasury, Marcy as Secretary of War, Mason as Attorney General, Cave Johnson as Postmaster General, and Bancroft as Secretary of the Navy. All would win Senate confirmation after Polk took office.</p>
<p>While Polk put together his Cabinet, President Tyler sought to complete the annexation of Texas. While the Senate had defeated an earlier treaty that would annex the republic, Tyler sought to pass a joint resolution through both houses of Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry120124_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry120124-77">[77]</a></sup> Due to disagreements regarding the extension of slavery, Senator Benton of Missouri and Secretary of State Calhoun disagreed on the best way to annex Texas, and Polk became involved in negotiations to break the impasse.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry120124_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry120124-77">[77]</a></sup> With Polk's help, the annexation resolution narrowly cleared the Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry120124_77-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry120124-77">[77]</a></sup> In a surprise move two days before Polk's inauguration, Tyler extended to Texas a formal offer of annexation.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry127128_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry127128-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_.281845.E2.80.931849.29">Presidency (1845–1849)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_K._Polk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Presidency (1845–1849)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_James_K._Polk" title="Presidency of James K. Polk">Presidency of James K. Polk</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Polk_inauguration_Oath_London_News.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Polk_inauguration_Oath_London_News.jpg/300px-Polk_inauguration_Oath_London_News.jpg" width="300" height="237" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Polk_inauguration_Oath_London_News.jpg/450px-Polk_inauguration_Oath_London_News.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Polk_inauguration_Oath_London_News.jpg/600px-Polk_inauguration_Oath_London_News.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3630" data-file-height="2870" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Polk_inauguration_Oath_London_News.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The inauguration of James K. Polk, as shown in the <i>Illustrated London News</i>, v. 6, April 19, 1845.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When he took office on March 4, 1845, Polk, at 49, became the youngest man at the time to assume the presidency. Polk's inauguration was the first <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_inauguration" title="United States presidential inauguration">inaugural ceremony</a> to be reported by <a href="/wiki/Telegraph" class="mw-redirect" title="Telegraph">telegraph</a> and to be shown in a newspaper illustration (in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Illustrated_London_News" title="The Illustrated London News">The Illustrated London News</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Polk presided over a country whose population had doubled every twenty years since the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a> and which had reached demographic parity with its former colonial overlord, <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry132133_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry132133-80">[80]</a></sup> Polk's tenure saw continued technological improvements, including the continued expansion of <a href="/wiki/Railroad" class="mw-redirect" title="Railroad">railroads</a> and increased use of the <a href="/wiki/Telegraph" class="mw-redirect" title="Telegraph">telegraph</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry132133_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry132133-80">[80]</a></sup> These improved communications and growing demographics increasingly made the United States into a strong military power, while also stoking <a href="/wiki/Expansionism" title="Expansionism">expansionism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry131132_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry131132-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to a story told decades later by <a href="/wiki/George_Bancroft" title="George Bancroft">George Bancroft</a>, Polk set four clearly defined goals for his administration:<sup id="cite_ref-Merry131132_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry131132-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Reestablish the <a href="/wiki/Independent_Treasury" title="Independent Treasury">Independent Treasury System</a>.</li>
<li>Reduce <a href="/wiki/Tariffs_in_American_history" class="mw-redirect" title="Tariffs in American history">tariffs</a>.</li>
<li>Acquire some or all of <a href="/wiki/Oregon_boundary_dispute" title="Oregon boundary dispute">Oregon Country</a>.</li>
<li>Acquire <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a> from <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>.</li>
</ul>
<p>While his domestic aims represented continuity with past Democratic policies, successful completion of Polk's foreign policy goals would represent the first major <a href="/wiki/United_States_territorial_acquisitions" title="United States territorial acquisitions">American territorial gains</a> since the <a href="/wiki/Adams%E2%80%93On%C3%ADs_Treaty" title="Adams–Onís Treaty">Adams–Onís Treaty</a> of 1819.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry131132_81-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry131132-81">[81]</a></sup> Pledged to serve only one term, Polk accomplished his four major objectives in just four years. By linking acquisition of new lands in Oregon (with no slavery) and Texas (with slavery), he hoped to satisfy both North and South.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>During his presidency James K. Polk was known as "Young Hickory", an allusion to his mentor <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>, and "Napoleon of the <a href="/wiki/Stump_speech_(politics)" title="Stump speech (politics)">Stump</a>" for his speaking skills.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personnel">Personnel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_K._Polk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Personnel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration_and_cabinet">Administration and cabinet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_K._Polk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Administration and cabinet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:State-dining-room-polk-cabinet.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/State-dining-room-polk-cabinet.jpg/310px-State-dining-room-polk-cabinet.jpg" width="310" height="242" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/State-dining-room-polk-cabinet.jpg/465px-State-dining-room-polk-cabinet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/82/State-dining-room-polk-cabinet.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="469" /></a>
