<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FFFFE0; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;After treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In May 1846, Taylor led the US in the inconclusive <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Palo_Alto" title="Battle of Palo Alto">Battle of Palo Alto</a>, the first major battle of the war.<sup id="cite_ref-merry259260_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry259260-121">[121]</a></sup> The next day, Taylor led the army to victory in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Resaca_de_la_Palma" title="Battle of Resaca de la Palma">Battle of Resaca de la Palma</a>, eliminating the possibility of a Mexican incursion into the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-merry259260_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry259260-121">[121]</a></sup> Meanwhile, <a href="/wiki/Winfield_Scott" title="Winfield Scott">Winfield Scott</a>, the army's lone <a href="/wiki/Major_general_(United_States)" title="Major general (United States)">major general</a> at the outbreak of the war, was offered the position of top commander in the war.<sup id="cite_ref-merry256257_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry256257-122">[122]</a></sup> Polk, War Secretary Marcy, and Scott agreed on a strategy in which the US would capture northern Mexico and then pursue a favorable peace settlement.<sup id="cite_ref-merry256257_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry256257-122">[122]</a></sup> However, Polk and Scott experienced mutual distrust from the beginning of their relationship, in part due to Scott's Whig affiliation and former rivalry with Andrew Jackson.<sup id="cite_ref-merry253254_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry253254-123">[123]</a></sup> Additionally, Polk sought to ensure that both Whigs and Democrats would serve in important positions in the war, and was offended when Scott suggested otherwise; Scott also angered Polk by opposing Polk's effort to increase the number of generals.<sup id="cite_ref-merry258259_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry258259-124">[124]</a></sup> Having been alienated from Scott, Polk ordered Scott to remain in Washington, leaving Taylor in command of Mexican operations.<sup id="cite_ref-merry259260_121-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry259260-121">[121]</a></sup> Polk also ordered Commodore Conner to allow Santa Anna to return to Mexico from his exile, and sent an army expedition led by <a href="/wiki/Stephen_W._Kearny" title="Stephen W. Kearny">Stephen W. Kearny</a> towards <a href="/wiki/Santa_Fe,_New_Mexico" title="Santa Fe, New Mexico">Santa Fe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1845, Polk, fearful of French or British intervention, had sent Lieutenant <a href="/wiki/Archibald_H._Gillespie" title="Archibald H. Gillespie">Archibald H. Gillespie</a> to California with orders to foment a pro-American rebellion that could be used to justify annexation of the territory.<sup id="cite_ref-merry295296_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry295296-126">[126]</a></sup> After meeting with Gillespie, Army captain <a href="/wiki/John_C._Fr%C3%A9mont" title="John C. Frémont">John C. Frémont</a> led settlers in northern California to overthrow the Mexican garrison in <a href="/wiki/Sonoma,_California" title="Sonoma, California">Sonoma</a> in what became known as the <a href="/wiki/California_Republic" title="California Republic">Bear Flag Revolt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-merry302304_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry302304-127">[127]</a></sup> In August 1846, American forces under Kearny captured Santa Fe, capital of the province of <a href="/wiki/Santa_Fe_de_Nuevo_M%C3%A9xico" title="Santa Fe de Nuevo México">New Mexico</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-merry293294_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry293294-128">[128]</a></sup> Kearny captured Santa Fe without firing a shot, after the Mexican Governor, <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Armijo" title="Manuel Armijo">Manuel Armijo</a>, fled from the province.<sup id="cite_ref-merry298299_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry298299-129">[129]</a></sup> At roughly the same time that Kearny captured Santa Fe, Commodore <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Stockton" title="Robert F. Stockton">Robert F. Stockton</a> landed in <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles,_California" class="mw-redirect" title="Los Angeles, California">Los Angeles</a> and proclaimed the capture of California.<sup id="cite_ref-merry293294_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry293294-128">[128]</a></sup> After American forces put down a revolt, United States held effective control of two northern Mexican provinces.<sup id="cite_ref-merry304306_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry304306-130">[130]</a></sup> However, the Western theater of the war would prove to be a political headache for Polk, as a dispute between Frémont and Kearny led to a break between Polk and the powerful Missouri Senator (and father-in-law of Frémont), Thomas Hart Benton.<sup id="cite_ref-merry396397_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry396397-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>At its outbreak, the war enjoyed support across the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-merry255256_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry255256-132">[132]</a></sup> However, Whig Congressman <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Delano" title="Columbus Delano">Columbus Delano</a> accused Polk of engineering the war, and Whig resistance to the war spread.<sup id="cite_ref-merry255256_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry255256-132">[132]</a></sup> In August 1846, Polk asked Congress to appropriate $2 million as a down payment for the potential purchase of California.<sup id="cite_ref-merry283285_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry283285-133">[133]</a></sup> Polk's request ignited opposition to the war, as Polk had never before made public his desire to annex parts of Mexico (aside from lands claimed by Texas).<sup id="cite_ref-merry283285_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry283285-133">[133]</a></sup> A freshman Democratic Congressman, <a href="/wiki/David_Wilmot" title="David Wilmot">David Wilmot</a> of Pennsylvania, offered an amendment to the bill that would ban slavery in any newly-acquired lands.<sup id="cite_ref-merry286289_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry286289-134">[134]</a></sup> The "<a href="/wiki/Wilmot_Proviso" title="Wilmot Proviso">Wilmot Proviso</a>," as it became known, injected the slavery debate into the Mexican-American War.<sup id="cite_ref-merry286289_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry286289-134">[134]</a></sup> The appropriation bill, with the Wilmot Proviso attached, passed the House in an 87–64 vote, but the bill died in the Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-merry290291_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry290291-135">[135]</a></sup> Polk's Democrats would pay a price for the resistance to the war and the growing issue of slavery, as Democrats lost control of the House in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections,_1846" title="United States House of Representatives elections, 1846">1846 elections</a>. However, in early 1847, Polk was successful in passing a bill raising further regiments, and he also finally won approval for the money he wanted to use for the purchase of California.<sup id="cite_ref-merry343349_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry343349-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>In July 1846, American envoy <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Slidell_Mackenzie" title="Alexander Slidell Mackenzie">Alexander Slidell Mackenzie</a> met with Santa Anna, offering terms in which the US would pay to acquire the <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Bay" title="San Francisco Bay">San Francisco Bay</a> and other parts of Alta California.<sup id="cite_ref-merry278279_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry278279-137">[137]</a></sup> Santa Anna returned to Mexico City in September 1846, declaring that he would fight against the Americans.<sup id="cite_ref-merry309310_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry309310-138">[138]</a></sup> With the duplicity of Santa Anna now clear, and with the Mexicans declining his peace offer, Polk ordered an American landing in <a href="/wiki/Veracruz_(city)" title="Veracruz (city)">Veracruz</a>, the most important Mexican port on the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-merry309310_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry309310-138">[138]</a></sup> In the September 1846 <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Monterrey" title="Battle of Monterrey">Battle of Monterrey</a>, Taylor defeated a Mexican force led by Ampudia, but allowed Ampudia's forces to withdraw, much to Polk's consternation.<sup id="cite_ref-merry311313_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry311313-139">[139]</a></sup> Taylor was ordered to remain near Monterrey, while Polk reluctantly chose Winfield Scott to lead the attack on Veracruz.<sup id="cite_ref-merry318320_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry318320-140">[140]</a></sup> Polk decided that Scott's force would land in Veracruz and then march on Mexico City.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> While Taylor defeated Santa Anna at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Buena_Vista" title="Battle of Buena Vista">Battle of Buena Vista</a>, Scott landed in Veracruz, and quickly <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Veracruz" title="Siege of Veracruz">won control</a> of the city.<sup id="cite_ref-merry352359_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry352359-142">[142]</a></sup> With the capture of Veracruz, Polk dispatched <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Trist" title="Nicholas Trist">Nicholas Trist</a>, Buchanan's chief clerk, to negotiate a peace treaty with Mexican leaders.<sup id="cite_ref-merry358359_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry358359-143">[143]</a></sup> Trist was ordered to seek the cession of Alta California, New Mexico, and <a href="/wiki/Baja_California_Peninsula" title="Baja California Peninsula">Baja California</a>, recognition of the Rio Grande as the southern border of Texas, and American access across the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Tehuantepec" title="Isthmus of Tehuantepec">Isthmus of Tehuantepec</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-merry360361_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry360361-144">[144]</a></sup> Trist was authorized to make a payment of up to $30 million in exchange for these concessions.<sup id="cite_ref-merry360361_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry360361-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>In August 1847, Scott defeated Santa Anna at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Contreras" title="Battle of Contreras">Battle of Contreras</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Churubusco" title="Battle of Churubusco">Battle of Churubusco</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-merry381382_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry381382-145">[145]</a></sup> With these victories over a larger force, Scott's army was positioned to besiege Mexico's capital.<sup id="cite_ref-merry381382_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry381382-145">[145]</a></sup> Santa Anna negotiated a truce with Scott, and the Mexican foreign minister notified Trist that they were ready to begin negotiations.<sup id="cite_ref-merry383384_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry383384-146">[146]</a></sup> However, the Mexican and American delegations remained far apart on terms; Mexico was only willing to yield portions of Upper California, and refused to agree to the Rio Grande border.<sup id="cite_ref-merry384385_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry384385-147">[147]</a></sup> While negotiations continued, Scott captured the Mexican capital in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_for_Mexico_City" title="Battle for Mexico City">Battle for Mexico City</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-merry387388_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry387388-148">[148]</a></sup> In the United States, a heated political debate emerged regarding how much of Mexico the United States should seek to annex, with Whigs such as Henry Clay arguing that the United States should only seek to settle the Texas border question, and some expansionists arguing for the <a href="/wiki/All_of_Mexico_Movement" title="All of Mexico Movement">annexation of all of Mexico</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-merry394397_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry394397-149">[149]</a></sup> Frustrated by the lack of progress in negotiations, and troubled by rumors that Trist was willing to negotiate on the Rio Grande border, Polk ordered Trist to return to Washington.<sup id="cite_ref-merry386_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry386-150">[150]</a></sup> Polk decided to occupy large portions of Mexico and wait for a Mexican peace offer.<sup id="cite_ref-merry403404_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry403404-151">[151]</a></sup> In late 1847, Polk learned of Scott and Trist's attempt to bribe Mexican officials in an attempt to open negotiations, as well as Scott's court-martial of a close Polk friend, <a href="/wiki/Gideon_Johnson_Pillow" title="Gideon Johnson Pillow">Gideon Johnson Pillow</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-merry407409_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry407409-152">[152]</a></sup> Outraged especially by the latter event, Polk demanded Scott's return to Washington, with <a href="/wiki/William_Orlando_Butler" title="William Orlando Butler">William Orlando Butler</a> tapped as his replacement.<sup id="cite_ref-merry407409_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry407409-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Peace:_the_Treaty_of_Guadalupe_Hidalgo">Peace: the Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_K._Polk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Peace: the Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Treaty_of_Guadalupe_Hidalgo.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Treaty_of_Guadalupe_Hidalgo.png/210px-Treaty_of_Guadalupe_Hidalgo.png" width="210" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Treaty_of_Guadalupe_Hidalgo.png/315px-Treaty_of_Guadalupe_Hidalgo.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/88/Treaty_of_Guadalupe_Hidalgo.png 2x" data-file-width="320" data-file-height="315" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Treaty_of_Guadalupe_Hidalgo.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Cession" title="Mexican Cession">Mexican Cession</a> (in red) was acquired through the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Guadalupe_Hidalgo" title="Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo">Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Gadsden_Purchase" title="Gadsden Purchase">Gadsden Purchase</a> (in orange) was acquired through purchase after Polk left office.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In September 1847, <a href="/wiki/Manuel_de_la_Pe%C3%B1a_y_Pe%C3%B1a" title="Manuel de la Peña y Peña">Manuel de la Peña y Peña</a> replaced Santa Anna as President of Mexico, and Pena and his Moderado allies showed a willingness to negotiate based on the terms Polk had relayed to Trist.<sup id="cite_ref-merry397400_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry397400-153">[153]</a></sup> In November 1847, Trist received Polk's order to return to Washington.<sup id="cite_ref-merry397400_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry397400-153">[153]</a></sup> After a period of indecision, and with the backing of Scott and the Mexican government (which was aware of Trist's recall), Trist decided to enter into negotiations with the Mexican government.<sup id="cite_ref-merry397400_153-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry397400-153">[153]</a></sup> As Polk had made no plans to send an envoy to replace him, Trist thought that he could not pass up the opportunity to end the war on favorable terms.<sup id="cite_ref-merry397400_153-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry397400-153">[153]</a></sup> Though Polk was outraged by Trist's decision, he decided to allow Trist some time to negotiate a treaty.<sup id="cite_ref-merry420421_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry420421-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>Throughout January 1848, Trist regularly met with Mexican officials in Guadalupe Hidalgo, a small town North of Mexico City.<sup id="cite_ref-merry424426_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry424426-155">[155]</a></sup> Trist was willing to allow Mexico to keep Lower California, but successfully haggled for the inclusion of the important harbor of <a href="/wiki/San_Diego" title="San Diego">San Diego</a> in a cession of Upper California.<sup id="cite_ref-merry424426_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry424426-155">[155]</a></sup> The Mexican delegation agreed to recognize the Rio Grande border, while Trist agreed to have the United States cover prior American claims against the Mexican government.<sup id="cite_ref-merry424426_155-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry424426-155">[155]</a></sup> The two sides also agreed to the right of Mexicans in annexed territory to leave or become U.S. citizens, American responsibility to prevent cross-border Indian raids, protection of church property, and a $15 million payment to Mexico.<sup id="cite_ref-merry424426_155-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry424426-155">[155]</a></sup> On February 2, 1848, Trist and the Mexican delegation signed the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Guadalupe_Hidalgo" title="Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo">Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-merry424426_155-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry424426-155">[155]</a></sup> Polk received the document on February 19.<sup id="cite_ref-merry426427_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry426427-156">[156]</a></sup> While he remained angry at Trist for disobeying orders, Polk and a majority of his Cabinet accepted the terms of the treaty.<sup id="cite_ref-merry426427_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry426427-156">[156]</a></sup> In dissent were Buchanan, who called for more territory, and Secretary of the Treasury Walker, who wanted to annex all of Mexico.<sup id="cite_ref-merry426427_156-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry426427-156">[156]</a></sup> Though Polk also expressed a desire for more territory, he was eager to bring the war to a close and reasonably satisfied with the amount of ceded territory, so he submitted the treaty to the Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-merry428429_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry428429-157">[157]</a></sup> Ratification of the treaty required a 2/3 super-majority of the Senate, and ratification was uncertain due to the opposition of various Senators, including those who opposed the annexation of any Mexican territory.<sup id="cite_ref-merry430431_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry430431-158">[158]</a></sup> On March 10, the Senate ratified the treaty in a 38–14 vote, with aye and nay votes spread equally across partisan and geographic lines.<sup id="cite_ref-merry434435_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry434435-159">[159]</a></sup> The Senate made some modifications to the treaty before ratification, and Polk worried that the Mexican government would reject the modifications.<sup id="cite_ref-merry436437_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry436437-160">[160]</a></sup> However, on June 7, Polk learned that Mexico had ratified the treaty, thus ending the war.<sup id="cite_ref-merry448450_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry448450-161">[161]</a></sup> With the acquisition of California, Polk had accomplished all four of his major presidential goals.<sup id="cite_ref-merry448450_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry448450-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Cession" title="Mexican Cession">Mexican Cession</a> added 600,000 square miles of territory to the United States, including a long <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a> coastline.<sup id="cite_ref-merry448450_161-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry448450-161">[161]</a></sup> The modern states of <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a>, most of <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>, and parts of <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a> were all included in the Mexican Cession. The treaty also recognized the annexation of Texas and acknowledged American control over the disputed territory between the Nueces River and the Rio Grande. Mexico, in turn, received $15&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-merry424426_155-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry424426-155">[155]</a></sup> The war had cost the lives of nearly 14,000 Americans and 25,000 Mexicans, as well as the expense of roughly one hundred million dollars.<sup id="cite_ref-merry448450_161-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry448450-161">[161]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> The 1853 <a href="/wiki/Gadsden_Purchase" title="Gadsden Purchase">Gadsden Purchase</a> would later acquire another 30,000 square miles from Mexico (the area eventually became part of Arizona and New Mexico), establishing the modern borders of the <a href="/wiki/Contiguous_United_States" title="Contiguous United States">Contiguous United States</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:United_States_1845-03-1845-12.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/United_States_1845-03-1845-12.png/400px-United_States_1845-03-1845-12.png" width="400" height="271" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/United_States_1845-03-1845-12.png/600px-United_States_1845-03-1845-12.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/United_States_1845-03-1845-12.png/800px-United_States_1845-03-1845-12.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="677" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:United_States_1845-03-1845-12.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
United States states and territories when Polk entered office</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:United_States_1849-1850.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/United_States_1849-1850.png/400px-United_States_1849-1850.png" width="400" height="271" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/United_States_1849-1850.png/600px-United_States_1849-1850.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/United_States_1849-1850.png/800px-United_States_1849-1850.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="677" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:United_States_1849-1850.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
United States states and territories when Polk left office</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Polk issued an official proclamation of the end of the war on July 4, 1848.<sup id="cite_ref-merry452453_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry452453-163">[163]</a></sup> Polk's next task was the establishment of territorial governments in the new territories, which was complicated by the ongoing slavery debate.<sup id="cite_ref-merry452453_163-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry452453-163">[163]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Compromise" title="Missouri Compromise">Missouri Compromise</a> had settled the issue of the geographic reach of slavery within the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</a> territories by prohibiting slavery North of the 36°30′ latitude, and Polk sought to extend this line into the newly-acquired territory.<sup id="cite_ref-merry452453_163-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry452453-163">[163]</a></sup> Polk supported a territorial organization bill crafted by Senator <a href="/wiki/John_M._Clayton" title="John M. Clayton">John M. Clayton</a> that would have prohibited slavery in Oregon while staying silent on the issue in California and New Mexico.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> Though the bill passed the Senate, it was defeated in the House in an effort led by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_H._Stephens" title="Alexander H. Stephens">Alexander H. Stephens</a> of Georgia.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> A separate measure to extend the Missouri Compromise line to the Pacific coast was also defeated in the House, this time by a bipartisan alliance of northerners.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> As the last congressional session before the 1848 election came to a close, Polk signed the lone territorial bill passed by Congress, which established the <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Territory" title="Oregon Territory">Territory of Oregon</a> and prohibited slavery in it.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> Polk feared that the failure to provide for an organized government in New Mexico and especially California could lead to independence for those remote regions.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry465466_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry465466-168">[168]</a></sup> When Congress re-convened in December, he focused his efforts on granting immediate statehood to California, thereby avoiding the issue of the Wilmot Proviso.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry465466_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry465466-168">[168]</a></sup> However, the divisive issue of slavery blocked any such legislation, and Polk left office fearing that the United States might lose the western territories acquired during his presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Compromise_of_1850" title="Compromise of 1850">Compromise of 1850</a> would later settle a number of outstanding issues from Polk's presidency, including the admission of California as a state and the creation of <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico_Territory" title="New Mexico Territory">New Mexico Territory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Utah_Territory" title="Utah Territory">Utah Territory</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_initiatives">Other initiatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_K._Polk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Other initiatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Polk's ambassador to the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_New_Granada" title="Republic of New Granada">Republic of New Granada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Alden_Bidlack" title="Benjamin Alden Bidlack">Benjamin Alden Bidlack</a>, negotiated the <a href="/wiki/Mallarino%E2%80%93Bidlack_Treaty" title="Mallarino–Bidlack Treaty">Mallarino–Bidlack Treaty</a> with the government of New Granada.<sup id="cite_ref-Conniff1_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conniff1-170">[170]</a></sup> Though Bidlack had initially only sought to remove tariffs on American goods, Bidlack and New Granadan Foreign Minister <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Mar%C3%ADa_Mallarino" title="Manuel María Mallarino">Manuel María Mallarino</a> negotiated a broader agreement that deepened military and trade ties between the two countries.<sup id="cite_ref-Conniff1_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conniff1-170">[170]</a></sup> The treaty also allowed for the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal_Railway" title="Panama Canal Railway">Panama Railway</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-randall1_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-randall1-171">[171]</a></sup> In an era of slow overland travel, the treaty gave the United States a route to more rapidly travel between its eastern and western coasts.<sup id="cite_ref-randall1_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-randall1-171">[171]</a></sup> In exchange, Bidlack agreed to have the United States guarantee New Granada's sovereignty over the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Panama" title="Isthmus of Panama">Isthmus of Panama</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Conniff1_170-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conniff1-170">[170]</a></sup> The treaty won ratification in both countries in 1848.<sup id="cite_ref-randall1_171-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-randall1-171">[171]</a></sup> The agreement helped to establish a stronger American influence in the region, as the Polk administration sought to ensure that Great Britain would not dominate <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-randall1_171-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-randall1-171">[171]</a></sup> The United States would use the Mallarino-Bidlack Treaty as justification for numerous military interventions in the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Conniff1_170-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conniff1-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<p>In mid-1848, President Polk authorized his ambassador to Spain, <a href="/wiki/Romulus_Mitchell_Saunders" title="Romulus Mitchell Saunders">Romulus Mitchell Saunders</a>, to negotiate the purchase of <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> and offer <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> up to $100&#160;million, an astounding sum at the time for one territory, equal to $2.77&#160;billion in present-day terms.<sup id="cite_ref-inflation-US_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-172">[172]</a></sup> Cuba was close to the United States and had slavery, so the idea appealed to Southerners but was unwelcome in the North. However, Spain was still making huge profits in Cuba (notably in sugar, molasses, rum, and tobacco), and thus the Spanish government rejected Saunders' overtures.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> Though Polk was eager to acquire Cuba, he refused to support the proposed <a href="/wiki/Filibuster_(military)" title="Filibuster (military)">filibuster</a> expedition of <a href="/wiki/Narciso_L%C3%B3pez" title="Narciso López">Narciso López</a>, who sought to invade and annex Cuba.<sup id="cite_ref-tchaffin1_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tchaffin1-174">[174]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_policy">Domestic policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_K._Polk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Domestic policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Knox_Polk_by_GPA_Healy,_1858.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/James_Knox_Polk_by_GPA_Healy%2C_1858.jpg/220px-James_Knox_Polk_by_GPA_Healy%2C_1858.jpg" width="220" height="290" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/James_Knox_Polk_by_GPA_Healy%2C_1858.jpg/330px-James_Knox_Polk_by_GPA_Healy%2C_1858.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/James_Knox_Polk_by_GPA_Healy%2C_1858.jpg/440px-James_Knox_Polk_by_GPA_Healy%2C_1858.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2275" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Knox_Polk_by_GPA_Healy,_1858.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Polk's official White House portrait, by <a href="/wiki/George_Peter_Alexander_Healy" title="George Peter Alexander Healy">George Peter Alexander Healy</a>, 1858</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fiscal_policy">Fiscal policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_K._Polk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Fiscal policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In his inaugural address, Polk called upon Congress to re-establish the <a href="/wiki/Independent_Treasury" title="Independent Treasury">Independent Treasury</a> System under which government funds were held in the Treasury and not in banks or other financial institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-merry206207_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry206207-175">[175]</a></sup> President Van Buren had previously established a similar system, but it had been abolished during the Tyler administration.<sup id="cite_ref-sieg121122_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sieg121122-176">[176]</a></sup> After personally winning the support of Senator <a href="/wiki/Dixon_Lewis" class="mw-redirect" title="Dixon Lewis">Dixon Lewis</a>, the chairman of the Senate Finance Committee, Polk was able to push the Independent Treasury Act through Congress, signing it into law on August 6, 1846.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry273_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry273-177">[177]</a></sup> The act provided that the public revenues were to be retained in the <a href="/wiki/Treasury_Building_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Treasury Building (Washington, D.C.)">Treasury building</a> and in sub-treasuries in various cities, separate from private or state banks, to receive all government funds.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry273_177-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry273-177">[177]</a></sup> The system would remain in place until the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve_Act" title="Federal Reserve Act">Federal Reserve Act</a> in 1913.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry276-277_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry276-277-178">[178]</a></sup></p>
<p>Polk's other major domestic initiative was the lowering of the tariff, as Polk argued that the tariff should only be as high as necessary for the operation of the federal government.<sup id="cite_ref-merry206207_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry206207-175">[175]</a></sup> Though he had taken an ambivalent position on the tariff during the 1844 campaign in order to win Northern votes, Polk had long opposed a high tariff and viewed the <a href="/wiki/Protectionism" title="Protectionism">protection</a> of manufacturing interests as unfair to other economic activities.<sup id="cite_ref-sieg113114_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sieg113114-179">[179]</a></sup> Polk directed Secretary of the Treasury Robert Walker to draft a new and lower tariff, which Polk submitted to Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-sieg113114_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sieg113114-179">[179]</a></sup> After intense lobbying campaigns by both sides, the bill passed the House and, in a close vote that required Vice President Dallas to <a href="/wiki/List_of_tie-breaking_votes_cast_by_vice_presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of tie-breaking votes cast by vice presidents of the United States">break a tie</a>, the Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-sieg115116_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sieg115116-180">[180]</a></sup> In 1846, Polk signed the <a href="/wiki/Walker_tariff" title="Walker tariff">Walker Tariff</a> into law, substantially reducing the rates that had been set by the Whig-backed <a href="/wiki/Tariff_of_1842" title="Tariff of 1842">Tariff of 1842</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> The new law abandoned <i><a href="/wiki/Ad_valorem_tax" title="Ad valorem tax">ad valorem</a></i> tariffs and set rates independent of the monetary value of the product.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry274276_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry274276-182">[182]</a></sup> Polk's lowering of the tariff was popular in the South and West; however, it was despised by many protectionists in the Northeast, especially <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry274276_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry274276-182">[182]</a></sup> A reduction of tariffs in the United States and a repeal of the <a href="/wiki/Corn_Laws" title="Corn Laws">Corn Laws</a> in Great Britain led to a boom in Anglo-American trade.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry276-277_178-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry276-277-178">[178]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rivers_and_Harbors_veto">Rivers and Harbors veto</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_K._Polk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Rivers and Harbors veto">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/Rivers_and_Harbors_Bill" title="Rivers and Harbors Bill">Rivers and Harbors Bill</a> in 1846 to provide $500,000 to improve rivers and harbors, but Polk vetoed the bill. It would have provided for federally funded <a href="/wiki/Internal_improvements" title="Internal improvements">internal improvements</a> on small harbors. Polk believed that this was unconstitutional because the bill unfairly favored particular areas, including ports which had no foreign trade. Polk believed that these problems were local and not national. Polk feared that passing the Rivers and Harbors Bill would encourage legislators to compete for favors for their home districts – a type of <a href="/wiki/Political_corruption" title="Political corruption">corruption</a> that would spell doom to the <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">virtue of the republic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> In this regard he followed his hero <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>, who had vetoed the <a href="/wiki/Maysville_Road_veto" title="Maysville Road veto">Maysville Road Bill</a> in 1830 on similar grounds.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Slavery">Slavery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_K._Polk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Slavery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Polk_restored.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/James_Polk_restored.jpg/180px-James_Polk_restored.jpg" width="180" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/James_Polk_restored.jpg/270px-James_Polk_restored.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/James_Polk_restored.jpg/360px-James_Polk_restored.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2079" data-file-height="2500" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Polk_restored.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Daguerreotype of Polk as President by <a href="/wiki/Mathew_Brady" title="Mathew Brady">Mathew Brady</a>, 1849</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Like Jackson, Polk saw slavery as a side issue compared to more important matters such as territorial expansion and economic policy.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry129130_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry129130-185">[185]</a></sup> However, the issue of slavery became increasingly polarizing during the 1840s, and Polk's expansionary policies increased its divisiveness.<sup id="cite_ref-Merry129130_185-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merry129130-185">[185]</a></sup> During his presidency, many abolitionists harshly criticized him as an instrument of the "<a href="/wiki/Slave_Power" title="Slave Power">Slave Power</a>", and claimed that spreading slavery was the reason he supported <a href="/wiki/Texas_Annexation" class="mw-redirect" title="Texas Annexation">annexing Texas</a> and later <a href="/wiki/Mexican%E2%80%93American_War" title="Mexican–American War">war with Mexico</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Wilmot_Proviso" title="Wilmot Proviso">Wilmot Proviso</a>, introduced by Democratic Representative <a href="/wiki/David_Wilmot" title="David Wilmot">David Wilmot</a> of Pennsylvania on August 8, 1846 (just two months after the outbreak of the Mexican–American War), aimed to ban slavery anywhere in any territory that might be acquired from Mexico. Polk and many other Southerners were against the measure (which passed in the House, but not in the Senate). Polk argued instead for extending the <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Compromise" title="Missouri Compromise">Missouri Compromise</a> line west to the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>. That would have allowed slavery below the 36° 30' latitude line west of <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a>, and prohibit it above.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> Though Polk opposed the Wilmot Proviso, he also condemned southern agitation on the issue, and he accused both northern and southern leaders of attempting to use the slavery issue for political gain.<sup id="cite_ref-merry356358_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry356358-188">[188]</a></sup></p>
<p>Polk was a slaveholder for his entire life. His father, Samuel Polk, had left Polk more than 8,000 acres (32&#160;km²) of land, and divided about 53 slaves to his widow and children after he died. James inherited twenty of his father's slaves, either directly or from deceased brothers. In 1831, he became an absentee cotton planter, sending slaves to clear <a href="/wiki/Plantations_in_the_American_South" title="Plantations in the American South">plantation</a> land that his father had left him near <a href="/wiki/Somerville,_Tennessee" title="Somerville, Tennessee">Somerville, Tennessee</a>. Four years later Polk sold his Somerville plantation and, together with his brother-in-law, bought 920 acres (3.7&#160;km²) of land, a cotton plantation near <a href="/wiki/Coffeeville,_Mississippi" title="Coffeeville, Mississippi">Coffeeville, Mississippi</a>. He ran this plantation for the rest of his life, eventually taking it over completely from his brother-in-law. Polk rarely sold slaves, although once he became President and could better afford it, he bought more. Polk's will stipulated that their slaves were to be freed after his wife Sarah had died. However, the 1863 <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_Proclamation" title="Emancipation Proclamation">Emancipation Proclamation</a> and the 1865 <a href="/wiki/Thirteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Thirteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Thirteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution</a> freed all remaining slaves in rebel states long before the death of his wife in 1891.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup></p>
<p>On March 4, 2017, tombstones for two of his slaves, Elias Polk and Matilda Polk, were replaced in the <a href="/wiki/Nashville_City_Cemetery" title="Nashville City Cemetery">Nashville City Cemetery</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-tennesseanslavesrecognized_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tennesseanslavesrecognized-190">[190]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Department_of_the_Interior">Department of the Interior</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_K._Polk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Department of the Interior">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>One of Polk's last acts as President was to sign the bill creating the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Interior" title="United States Department of the Interior">Department of the Interior</a> (March 3, 1849). This was the first new cabinet position created since the early days of the Republic. Polk had misgivings about the federal government usurping power over public lands from the states; however, the delivery of the legislation on his last full day in office gave him no time to find constitutional grounds for a veto, or to draft a sufficient veto message, so Polk signed the bill.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1848">Election of 1848</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_K._Polk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Election of 1848">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1848" title="United States presidential election, 1848">United States presidential election, 1848</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1848_Electoral_Map.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/1848_Electoral_Map.png/350px-1848_Electoral_Map.png" width="350" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/1848_Electoral_Map.png/525px-1848_Electoral_Map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/1848_Electoral_Map.png/700px-1848_Electoral_Map.png 2x" data-file-width="868" data-file-height="635" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1848_Electoral_Map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Results of the 1848 Presidential election</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Honoring his pledge to serve only one term, Polk declined to seek re-election in 1848. However, Polk's actions strongly affected the 1848 race. With Polk out of the race, the Democratic Party remained fractured along geographic lines, with Senators Cass and Calhoun the leading western and southern candidates, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-merry376377_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry376377-192">[192]</a></sup> Former President Van Buren also retained strong support among northeasterners.<sup id="cite_ref-merry376377_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry376377-192">[192]</a></sup> Polk privately favored Cass as his successor, but resisted becoming closely involved in the election.<sup id="cite_ref-merry430431_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry430431-158">[158]</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/1848_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1848 Democratic National Convention">1848 Democratic National Convention</a>, which lasted from May 22 to May 25, a message from Polk reiterated his absolute refusal to serve a second term.<sup id="cite_ref-merry446-447_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry446-447-193">[193]</a></sup> At the convention, Buchanan, Cass, and Supreme Court Justice Levi Woodbury emerged as the main contenders.<sup id="cite_ref-merry446-447_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry446-447-193">[193]</a></sup> Cass led after the first ballot, and slowly gained support until he clinched the nomination on the fourth ballot.<sup id="cite_ref-merry446-447_193-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry446-447-193">[193]</a></sup> William Butler, who had replaced Winfield Scott as the commanding general in Mexico City, won the vice presidential nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-merry446-447_193-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry446-447-193">[193]</a></sup> Cass's nomination from many northerners and southerners, each of whom saw Cass as insufficiently committed to their position on the slavery issue.<sup id="cite_ref-merry446-447_193-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry446-447-193">[193]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the course of the Mexican War, Generals Taylor and Scott emerged as strong Whig candidates, alongside anti-war Senator <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Corwin" title="Thomas Corwin">Thomas Corwin</a> and longtime party leader Henry Clay.<sup id="cite_ref-merry374375_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry374375-194">[194]</a></sup> As the war continued, Taylor's stature with the public grew, and he announced in 1847 that he would not refuse the presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-merry374375_194-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry374375-194">[194]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/1848_Whig_National_Convention" title="1848 Whig National Convention">1848 Whig National Convention</a> took place on June 8, with Taylor, Clay, Scott, and Massachusetts Senator <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Webster" title="Daniel Webster">Daniel Webster</a> emerging as the major candidates.<sup id="cite_ref-merry447-448_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry447-448-195">[195]</a></sup> Taylor narrowly led Clay after the first ballot, but Taylor steadily grew his support until he captured the nomination on the fourth ballot.<sup id="cite_ref-merry447-448_195-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry447-448-195">[195]</a></sup> Clay bemoaned the selection of Taylor, as the ideologically ambiguous Taylor had not articulated his preferred policies.<sup id="cite_ref-merry447-448_195-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry447-448-195">[195]</a></sup> The Whigs chose former Congressman <a href="/wiki/Millard_Fillmore" title="Millard Fillmore">Millard Fillmore</a> of New York as Taylor's running mate.<sup id="cite_ref-merry447-448_195-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry447-448-195">[195]</a></sup></p>
<p>In New York, an anti-slavery Democratic faction known as the <a href="/wiki/Barnburners_and_Hunkers" title="Barnburners and Hunkers">Barnburners</a> strongly supported the Wilmot Proviso and rejected Cass.<sup id="cite_ref-merry455456_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry455456-196">[196]</a></sup> Joined by other anti-slavery Democrat from other states, the Barnburners held a convention nominating former President <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Martin Van Buren</a> as their own presidential nominee.<sup id="cite_ref-merry455456_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry455456-196">[196]</a></sup> Though Van Buren had not been known for his anti-slavery views while president, he embraced them in 1848.<sup id="cite_ref-merry455456_196-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry455456-196">[196]</a></sup> Polk was surprised and disappointed by his former ally's political conversion, and worried about the divisiveness of a sectional party organized around abolitionism.<sup id="cite_ref-merry455456_196-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry455456-196">[196]</a></sup> Van Buren was joined on the <a href="/wiki/Free_Soil_Party" title="Free Soil Party">Free Soil Party</a>'s ticket by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Francis_Adams_Sr." title="Charles Francis Adams Sr.">Charles Francis Adams Sr.</a>, son of former President and prominent Whig <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a>.</p>
<p>In the election, Taylor won 47.3% of the popular vote and a majority of the electoral vote, giving the Whigs control of the presidency. Cass won 42.5% of the vote, while Van Buren finished with 10.1% of the popular vote, more than any other <a href="/wiki/List_of_third_party_performances_in_United_States_presidential_elections" title="List of third party performances in United States presidential elections">third party presidential candidate</a> at that time. Despite the increasingly polarizing slavery debate, Taylor and Cass both won a mix of northern and southern states. However, much of Van Buren's support came from northern Democrats.<sup id="cite_ref-merry462-463_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry462-463-197">[197]</a></sup> Polk was very disappointed by the outcome as he had a low opinion of Taylor, seeing the general as someone with poor judgment and few opinions.<sup id="cite_ref-merry462-463_197-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merry462-463-197">[197]</a></sup> Polk left Washington on March 6, 1849, never to return.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-198">[198]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="States_admitted_to_the_Union">States admitted to the Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_K._Polk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: States admitted to the Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>&#160;– December 29, 1845</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a>&#160;– December 28, 1846</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>&#160;– May 29, 1848</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-presidency">Post-presidency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_K._Polk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Post-presidency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_K._Polk,_1847-1849.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/James_K._Polk%2C_1847-1849.jpg/220px-James_K._Polk%2C_1847-1849.jpg" width="220" height="268" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/James_K._Polk%2C_1847-1849.jpg/330px-James_K._Polk%2C_1847-1849.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/James_K._Polk%2C_1847-1849.jpg/440px-James_K._Polk%2C_1847-1849.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1008" data-file-height="1228" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_K._Polk,_1847-1849.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An undated daguerreotype of James K. Polk, from late in his presidential term or perhaps in the months after he left office</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Polk_Grave.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/James_Polk_Grave.jpg/220px-James_Polk_Grave.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/James_Polk_Grave.jpg/330px-James_Polk_Grave.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/James_Polk_Grave.jpg/440px-James_Polk_Grave.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Polk_Grave.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
James K. Polk's tomb lies on the grounds of the state capitol in Nashville, Tennessee.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Polk's time in the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> took its toll on his health. Full of enthusiasm and vigor when he entered office, Polk left on March 4, 1849, exhausted by his years of public service. He lost weight and had deep lines on his face and dark circles under his eyes. He is believed to have contracted <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, on a goodwill tour of the South after leaving the White House.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> He died of cholera at his new home,<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Polk_Place" title="Polk Place">Polk Place</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Nashville,_Tennessee" title="Nashville, Tennessee">Nashville, Tennessee</a>, at 3:15&#160;pm on June 15, 1849, three months after leaving office. He had never joined any church, but received a deathbed Methodist baptism.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> Initially buried in what is now <a href="/wiki/Nashville_City_Cemetery" title="Nashville City Cemetery">Nashville City Cemetery</a>, due to a legal requirement related to his infectious disease death, he was moved to a tomb on the grounds of Polk Place (as specified in his will) less than a year later.<sup id="cite_ref-Burke2017_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burke2017-202">[202]</a></sup> Polk's last words illustrate his devotion to his wife: "I love you, Sarah. For all eternity, I love you."<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup> She lived at Polk Place for 42 years after his death and died on August 14, 1891. His mother, Jane Knox Polk, died on January 11, 1852.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> In 1893, the bodies of President and Mrs. Polk were exhumed and relocated to their current resting place on the grounds of the <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_State_Capitol" title="Tennessee State Capitol">Tennessee State Capitol</a> in Nashville. Polk Place was demolished in 1900. In 2017, state legislative action was considered to relocate the Polk's remains to <a href="/wiki/James_K._Polk_Ancestral_Home" title="James K. Polk Ancestral Home">his father's home</a> in Columbia, Tennessee.<sup id="cite_ref-Burke2017_202-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burke2017-202">[202]</a></sup></p>
<p>Polk had <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Presidents_by_age" class="mw-redirect" title="List of United States Presidents by age">the shortest retirement</a> of all Presidents at 103 days.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> He was the youngest former president to die in retirement at the age of 53 (only <a href="/wiki/James_A._Garfield" title="James A. Garfield">James A. Garfield</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a>, who were both assassinated in office, died at a younger age). Along with <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Johnson" title="Andrew Johnson">Andrew Johnson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chester_A._Arthur" title="Chester A. Arthur">Chester A. Arthur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge" title="Calvin Coolidge">Calvin Coolidge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a>, he is one of six presidents to have died while his direct successor was in office.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reputation">Reputation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_K._Polk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Reputation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mexican%E2%80%93American_War#Opposition_to_the_war" title="Mexican–American War">Mexican–American War §&#160;Opposition to the war</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Knox_Polk_Statue.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/James_Knox_Polk_Statue.JPG/170px-James_Knox_Polk_Statue.JPG" width="170" height="387" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/James_Knox_Polk_Statue.JPG/255px-James_Knox_Polk_Statue.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/James_Knox_Polk_Statue.JPG/340px-James_Knox_Polk_Statue.JPG 2x" data-file-width="968" data-file-height="2201" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Knox_Polk_Statue.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A statue of Polk at the <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina_State_Capitol" title="North Carolina State Capitol">North Carolina State Capitol</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Polk's historic reputation was largely formed by the attacks made on him in his own time. Whig politicians claimed that he was drawn from a well-deserved obscurity. <a href="/wiki/Sam_Houston" title="Sam Houston">Sam Houston</a> is said to have observed that Polk was "a victim of the use of water as a beverage".<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> Senator <a href="/wiki/Tom_Corwin" class="mw-redirect" title="Tom Corwin">Tom Corwin</a> of Ohio remarked "James K. Polk, of Tennessee? <i>After that</i>, who is safe?" The Republican historians of the nineteenth century inherited this view. Polk was a compromise between the Democrats of the North, like <a href="/wiki/David_Wilmot" title="David Wilmot">David Wilmot</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silas_Wright" title="Silas Wright">Silas Wright</a>, and Southern plantation owners led by <a href="/wiki/John_C._Calhoun" title="John C. Calhoun">John C. Calhoun</a>. The Northern Democrats thought that when they did not get their way, it was because he was the tool of the slaveholders, and the conservatives of the South insisted that he was the tool of the Northern Democrats. These views were long reflected in the historical literature, until <a href="/wiki/Arthur_M._Schlesinger_Jr." title="Arthur M. Schlesinger Jr.">Arthur M. Schlesinger Jr.</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bernard_De_Voto" class="mw-redirect" title="Bernard De Voto">Bernard De Voto</a> argued that Polk was nobody's tool, but set his own goals and achieved them.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup></p>
<p>Polk is now recognized, not only as the strongest president between <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>, but also the president who made the United States a coast-to-coast nation. When historians began ranking the presidents in 1948, Polk ranked 10th in <a href="/wiki/Arthur_M._Schlesinger" class="mw-redirect" title="Arthur M. Schlesinger">Arthur M. Schlesinger</a>'s poll. and has subsequently ranked 8th in Schlesinger's 1962 poll, 11th in the Riders-McIver Poll (1996), 11th in the most recent Siena Poll (2002), 9th in the most recent <i>Wall Street Journal</i> Poll (2005), and 12th in the latest C-Span Poll (2009). He is usually among the highest-ranking one-term presidents.</p>
<p>Polk biographers over the years have sized up the magnitude of Polk's achievements and his legacy, particularly his two most recent. "There are three key reasons why James K. Polk deserves recognition as a significant and influential American president," Walter Borneman wrote. "First, Polk accomplished the objectives of his presidential term as he defined them; second, he was the most decisive chief executive before the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a>; and third, he greatly expanded the executive power of the presidency, particularly its war powers, its role as commander-in-chief, and its oversight of the executive branch."<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> President <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a> summarized this view by saying that Polk was "a great president. Said what he intended to do and did it."<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup></p>
<p>While Polk's legacy thus takes many forms, the most outstanding is the map of the <a href="/wiki/Contiguous_United_States" title="Contiguous United States">continental United States</a>, whose landmass he increased by a third. "To look at that map," <a href="/wiki/Robert_W._Merry" title="Robert W. Merry">Robert W. Merry</a> concluded, "and to take in the western and southwestern expanse included in it, is to see the magnitude of Polk's presidential accomplishments."<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> Though there were powerful forces compelling Americans to the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>, some historians, such as Gary Kornblith, have posited that a Clay presidency would have seen the permanent independence of Texas and California.<sup id="cite_ref-kornblith1_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kornblith1-212">[212]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nevertheless, Polk's aggressive expansionism has been criticized on ethical grounds. He believed in "<a href="/wiki/Manifest_Destiny" class="mw-redirect" title="Manifest Destiny">Manifest Destiny</a>" even more than most did. Referencing the Mexican–American War, General <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">Ulysses S. Grant</a> stated that "I was bitterly opposed to the [Texas annexation], and to this day regard the war, which resulted, as one of the most unjust ever waged by a stronger against a weaker nation. It was an instance of a republic following the bad example of European monarchies, in not considering justice in their desire to acquire additional territory."<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> Whig politicians, including <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a>, contended that the <a href="/wiki/Texas_Annexation" class="mw-redirect" title="Texas Annexation">Texas Annexation</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Cession" title="Mexican Cession">Mexican Cession</a> enhanced the <a href="/wiki/The_Slave_Power" class="mw-redirect" title="The Slave Power">pro-slavery factions</a> of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> Disputes pertaining to the status of slavery in the territories acquired during the Polk administration led to the <a href="/wiki/Compromise_of_1850" title="Compromise of 1850">Compromise of 1850</a>, one of the primary factors in the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a> and later the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-merry246247-119"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-merry246247_119-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-merry246247_119-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Merry, pp. 246–247</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-120"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-120">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In January 1848, the Whigs won a House vote attacking Polk in an amendment to a resolution praising Major General Taylor for his service in a "war unnecessarily and unconstitutionally begun by the President of the United States". <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://memory.loc.gov/cgi-bin/query/r?ammem/hlaw:@field(DOCID+@lit(hj04321))">House Journal, 30th Session (1848) pp. 183–184</a> The resolution, however, died in committee.</span></li>

"Rethinking the Coming of the Civil War: a Counterfactual Exercise". <i>Journal of American History</i> 90.1 (2003): 76–105. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Serial_Number" title="International Standard Serial Number">ISSN</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/search?fq=x0:jrnl&amp;q=n2:0021-8723">0021-8723</a>. Asks what if Polk had not gone to war?</li>
<li>Leonard, Thomas M. <i>James K. Polk: A Clear and Unquestionable Destiny</i>. 2000. <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0842026479" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 0-8420-2647-9</a>.</li>
<li>McCormac, Eugene Irving. <i>James K. Polk: A Political Biography to the End of a Career, 1845–1849</i>. Univ. of California Press, 1922. (1995 reprint has <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/094570710X" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 0-945707-10-X</a>.) hostile to Jacksonians</li>
